            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Sano hit the ground running and then some in his first big league tour of action, obliterating opposing pitchers to a .269/.385/.530 line while striking out a ton and yet somehow still showing pretty good grasp of the strike zone. Sano was a Three True Outcome superstar from the get-go, as he walked or struck out in more than half of his plate appearances last season while homering 18 times in just 335 trips to the plate. The strikeout rate was an alarming 35.5%, but from watching him regularly, it appeared clear that those weren't mistakes of opposing pitchers abusing or overwhelming him, but aggression inside the strike zone. The 15.8% walk rate would seem to agree. Nevertheless, if this is the kind of line he can post while learning the league, the world is going to be a scary, scary place because Miguel Sano is in it. Be afraid. (Brandon Warne)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Sano could have plenty of value in leagues where he carries third base eligibility, assuming the Twins' plan to put him out in the outfield comes to fruition. If they end up trading Trevor Plouffe -- they publicly say they won't -- then this could happen. Either way, his bat is special enough to make him an early-round selection. Someone else might even reach in your draft. If you can get him at a place you feel comfortable, go for it. He's a special, special talent.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Sano took a significant step back in every meaningful way in 2016. It'd be easy to point to him being shoehorned into right field as the primary culprit, but there was some worry when he came into camp not in the shape the team requested, and the season really never got off the ground from there. Sano hit just .236/.319/.462 and wasn't markedly better after a hamstring injury cost him all of June. Prior to the injury, Sano was playing right field nearly every day and hit .235/.341/.458. Upon returning, Sano stayed on the dirt and hit just .236/.303/.465 the rest of the way. It's certainly possible that the move to right sidetracked his entire season, but it's also possible that he was exposed as a power hitter with too much swing-and-miss in his game — something he'll have to adjust moving forward. He's working out hard — and has lost eight pounds this winter, according to a Twins source — to make the full-time shift back to third base, which should be positive for his fantasy and real-life futures. Having outfield eligibility this season in many, if not all leagues should also make him more sought after. It's possible some of the shine has worn off, but it's pretty unlikely he's going to have obscene buy-low potential in leagues this season. (@Brandon_Warne)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Buying low on Sano might be tough this spring with multi-position eligibility and big-time talent, but even paying a moderately high price for the young slugger could pay huge dividends. Monitor his stock into the spring, because there's potential for a huge payoff here.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
